   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Rockwell B-1 Lancer
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>

     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Rockwell
      </b>
      (now part of
      <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
       Boeing
      </a>
      )
      <b>
       B-1 Lancer
      </b>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        N 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      is a four-engine supersonic
      <a href="/wiki/Variable-sweep_wing" title="Variable-sweep wing">
       variable-sweep wing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">
       jet-powered
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Heavy_bomber" title="Heavy bomber">
       heavy
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bomber" title="Strategic bomber">
       strategic bomber
      </a>
      used by the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
       United States Air Force
      </a>
      (USAF). It was first envisioned in the 1960s as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">
       supersonic
      </a>
      bomber with
      <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">
       Mach
      </a>
      2 speed, and sufficient
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_(aircraft)" title="Range (aircraft)">
       range
      </a>
      and payload to replace the
      <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">
       Boeing B-52 Stratofortress
      </a>
      . It was developed into the B-1B, primarily a
      <a href="/wiki/Nap-of-the-earth" title="Nap-of-the-earth">
       low-level
      </a>
      penetrator with long range and Mach 1.25 speed capability at high altitude.
     </p>
     <p>
      Designed by
      <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_International" title="Rockwell International">
       Rockwell International
      </a>
      , development was delayed multiple times over its history due to changes in the perceived need for manned bombers. The initial B-1A version was developed in the early 1970s, but its production was canceled, and only four prototypes were built. The need for a new platform once again surfaced in the early 1980s, and the aircraft resurfaced as the B-1B version with the focus on low-level penetration bombing. However, by this point, development of
      <a href="/wiki/Stealth_technology" title="Stealth technology">
       stealth technology
      </a>
      was promising an aircraft of dramatically improved capability. Production went ahead as the B version would be operational before the "Advanced Technology Bomber" (which became the
      <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_B-2_Spirit" title="Northrop Grumman B-2 Spirit">
       B-2 Spirit
      </a>
      ), during a period when the B-52 would be increasingly vulnerable. The B-1B entered service in 1986 with the USAF
      <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">
       Strategic Air Command
      </a>
      (SAC) as a nuclear bomber.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the early 1990s, following the
      <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">
       Gulf War
      </a>
      and concurrent with the disestablishment of SAC and its reassignment to the newly formed
      <a href="/wiki/Air_Combat_Command" title="Air Combat Command">
       Air Combat Command
      </a>
      (ACC), the B-1B was converted to conventional bombing use. It first served in combat during
      <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Iraq_(1998)" title="Bombing of Iraq (1998)">
       Operation Desert Fox
      </a>
      in 1998 and again during the
      <a href="/wiki/NATO_bombing_of_Yugoslavia" title="NATO bombing of Yugoslavia">
       NATO action in Kosovo
      </a>
      the following year. The B-1B has supported U.S. and
      <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">
       NATO
      </a>
      military forces in
      <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001present)">
       Afghanistan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">
       Iraq
      </a>
      . The Lancer is the supersonic component of the USAF's long-range bomber force, along with the subsonic B-52 and B-2. The bomber is commonly called the "Bone" (originally from "B-One"). With the retirement of the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics/Grumman_EF-111A_Raven" title="General Dynamics/Grumman EF-111A Raven">
       General Dynamics/Grumman EF-111A Raven
      </a>
      in 1998 and the
      <a href="/wiki/Grumman_F-14_Tomcat" title="Grumman F-14 Tomcat">
       Grumman F-14 Tomcat
      </a>
      in 2006, the B-1B is the U.S. military's only active variable-sweep wing aircraft. The B-1B is expected to continue to serve into the 2030s, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Next-Generation_Bomber" title="Next-Generation Bomber">
       Long Range Strike Bomber
      </a>
      to start supplementing the B-1B in 2030.
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">
       Development
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_XB-70_Valkyrie" title="North American XB-70 Valkyrie">
       North American XB-70 Valkyrie
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1955 the U.S. Air Force issued requirements for a new bomber combining the payload and range of the
      <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">
       Boeing B-52 Stratofortress
      </a>
      with the Mach2 maximum speed of the
      <a href="/wiki/Convair_B-58_Hustler" title="Convair B-58 Hustler">
       Convair B-58 Hustler
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_99_p10_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_99_p10-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In December 1957, the U.S. Air Force selected
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">
       North American Aviation
      </a>
      's proposal to replace the B-52 with the
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_XB-70_Valkyrie" title="North American XB-70 Valkyrie">
       B-70 Valkyrie
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p12-13_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p12-13-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Valkyrie was a six-engine bomber that could reach
      <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">
       Mach
      </a>
      3 speeds at high altitude (70,000ft or 21,000m)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p15-7_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p15-7-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to avoid
      <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">
       interceptor aircraft
      </a>
      , the only effective anti-bomber weapon in the 1950s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.118_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.118-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soviet aircraft were already unable to intercept the high-flying
      <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">
       Lockheed U-2
      </a>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the Valkyrie would fly at similar altitudes but much higher speeds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.118_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.118-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In combat, the B-70 was expected to simply fly right by the defenders.
     </p>
     <p>
      By the late 1950s, however, anti-aircraft
      <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile" title="Surface-to-air missile">
       surface-to-air missiles
      </a>
      (SAMs) could threaten high-altitude aircraft,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_1999_p21_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_1999_p21-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as demonstrated by the
      <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">
       1960 downing
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gary_Powers" title="Gary Powers">
       Gary Powers
      </a>
      's U-2.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The USAF Strategic Air Command (SAC) began moving its bombers to low-level penetration before the U-2 downing. This tactic greatly reduces radar detection distances by use of
      <a href="/wiki/Terrain_mask#Terrain_masking" title="Terrain mask">
       terrain masking
      </a>
      ; using features of the terrain like hills and valleys, the line-of-sight from the radar to the bomber can be broken, rendering the radar (and human observers) incapable of seeing the target.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p6-8_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p6-8-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even at somewhat higher altitudes, radar systems of the era were subject to "
      <a href="/wiki/Clutter_(radar)" title="Clutter (radar)">
       clutter
      </a>
      " from stray returns from the ground and other objects, requiring a minimum angle above the ground to be effective. Bombers flying at low altitudes could remain under these angles simply by flying around the radar sites. This combination of effects made SAMs of the era ineffective against low-flying aircraft.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p6-8_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p6-8-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.119_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.119-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The same effects also meant that low flying aircraft were difficult to detect by higher flying
      <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">
       interceptor aircraft
      </a>
      , since their radar systems could not readily pick out opposing aircraft against the clutter from ground reflections (lack of
      <a href="/wiki/Look-down/shoot-down" title="Look-down/shoot-down">
       look-down/shoot-down
      </a>
      capability).
     </p>
     <p>
      The switch from high-altitude to low-altitude flight profiles severely affected the B-70, whose design was highly tuned to provide the desired high-altitude performance. Planners outlined a series of low-level profiles for the B-70, but higher
      <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_drag" title="Aerodynamic drag">
       aerodynamic drag
      </a>
      at low level limited the B-70 to subsonic speed while dramatically decreasing its range.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_1999_p21_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_1999_p21-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The result would be an aircraft with somewhat higher subsonic speed, but less range than the B-52 it was meant to replace. Unsuited for the new low-altitude role, and because of a growing shift to the
      <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">
       intercontinental ballistic missile
      </a>
      (ICBM) force, the B-70 bomber program was canceled in 1961 by President
      <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
       John F. Kennedy
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.118_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.118-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the two XB-70 prototypes were used in a supersonic research program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p14-6_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p14-6-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although never intended for the low-level role, the B-52's flexibility allowed it to outlast its intended successor as the nature of the air war environment changed. The B-52's huge fuel load allowed it to operate at lower altitudes for longer times, and the large airframe allowed the addition of improved
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_jamming_and_deception" title="Radar jamming and deception">
       radar jamming and deception
      </a>
      suites to deal with radars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the
      <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">
       Vietnam War
      </a>
      the concept that all future wars would be nuclear was turned on its head, and the "big belly" modifications increased the B-52's total bomb load to 60,000 pounds (27,000kg),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      turning it into a powerful tactical aircraft which could be used against ground troops along with strategic targets from high altitudes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.119_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.119-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The much smaller bomb bay of the B-70 would have made it much less useful in this role.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Design_studies_and_delays">
       Design studies and delays
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design studies and delays">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Although effective, the B-52 was not ideal for the low-level role. This led to a number of aircraft designs known as "penetrators", which were tuned specifically for long-range low-altitude flight. The first of these designs to see operation was the supersonic
      <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-111_Aardvark" title="General Dynamics F-111 Aardvark">
       F-111
      </a>
      fighter-bomber, which used variable-sweep wings for tactical missions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similar aircraft also emerged for other users as well, notably the
      <a href="/wiki/BAC_TSR-2" title="BAC TSR-2">
       BAC TSR-2
      </a>
      , and later,
      <a href="/wiki/Panavia_Tornado" title="Panavia Tornado">
       Panavia Tornado
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-24" title="Sukhoi Su-24">
       Sukhoi Su-24
      </a>
      . A number of studies on a strategic-range counterpart followed.
     </p>
     <p>
      The first post-B-70 strategic penetrator study was known as the
      <i>
       Subsonic Low Altitude Bomber
      </i>
      (SLAB), which was completed in 1961. This produced a design that looked more like an airliner than a bomber, with a large swept wing,
      <a href="/wiki/T-tail" title="T-tail">
       T-tail
      </a>
      and large
      <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">
       high-bypass engines
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was followed by the similar
      <i>
       Extended Range Strike Aircraft
      </i>
      (ERSA), which added a
      <a href="/wiki/Variable-sweep_wing" title="Variable-sweep wing">
       variable-sweep wing
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Planform" title="Planform">
       planform
      </a>
      , then in vogue in the aviation industry. ERSA envisioned a relatively small aircraft with a 10,000 pounds (4,500kg) payload and a range of 8,750 nautical miles (16,200 kilometres), with 2,500nmi (4,600km) being flown at low altitudes. In August 1963 the similar
      <i>
       Low-Altitude Manned Penetrator
      </i>
      (LAMP) design was completed, which called for an aircraft with a 20,000 pounds (9,100kg) bomb load and somewhat shorter range of 7,150nmi (13,240km).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_pp.575-6_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_pp.575-6-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      These all culminated in the October 1963
      <i>
       Advanced Manned Precision Strike System
      </i>
      (AMPSS), which led to industry studies at
      <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">
       Boeing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">
       General Dynamics
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">
       North American
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.576_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.576-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In mid-1964, the USAF had revised its requirements and retitled the project as
      <i>
       Advanced Manned Strategic Aircraft
      </i>
      (AMSA), which differed from AMPSS primarily in that it also demanded a high-speed high-altitude capability, similar to that of the existing Mach 2 class
      <a href="/wiki/Convair_B-58_Hustler" title="Convair B-58 Hustler">
       Convair B-58 Hustler
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.575_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.575-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Given the lengthy series of design studies, Rockwell engineers joked that the new name actually stood for "America's Most Studied Aircraft".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-study_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-study-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p23_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p23-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The arguments that led to the cancellation of the
      <a href="/wiki/North_American_XB-70_Valkyrie" title="North American XB-70 Valkyrie">
       B-70 program
      </a>
      had led some to question the need for a new strategic bomber of any sort. The U.S. Air Force was adamant about retaining bombers as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_triad" title="Nuclear triad">
       nuclear triad
      </a>
      concept that included bombers, ICBMs, and
      <a href="/wiki/Submarine-launched_ballistic_missile" title="Submarine-launched ballistic missile">
       submarine-launched ballistic missiles
      </a>
      (SLBMs) in a combined package that complicated any potential defense. They argued that the bomber was needed to attack hardened military targets and to provide a safe
      <a href="/wiki/Counterforce" title="Counterforce">
       counterforce
      </a>
      option because the bombers could be quickly launched into safe loitering areas where they could not be attacked. However, the introduction of the SLBM mooted the mobility and survivability argument, and a newer generation of ICBMs (
      <a href="/wiki/LGM-30_Minuteman" title="LGM-30 Minuteman">
       Minuteman III
      </a>
      had the accuracy and speed needed to attack point targets. During this time, ICBMs were seen as a less costly option based on their lower unit cost,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but development costs were much higher.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_1999_p21_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_1999_p21-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_Defense" title="Secretary of Defense">
       Secretary of Defense
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">
       Robert McNamara
      </a>
      preferred ICBMs over bombers for the Air Force portion of the deterrent force
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_pp.576-7_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_pp.576-7-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and felt a new expensive bomber was not needed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fas-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.576-8_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.576-8-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      McNamara limited the AMSA program to studies and component development beginning in 1964.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.576-8_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.576-8-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Program studies continued;
      <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">
       IBM
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Autonetics" title="Autonetics">
       Autonetics
      </a>
      were awarded AMSA advanced avionics study contracts in 1968.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.576-8_28-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.576-8-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p23-6_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p23-6-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      McNamara remained opposed to the program in favor of upgrading the existing
      <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-52_Stratofortress" title="Boeing B-52 Stratofortress">
       B-52
      </a>
      fleet and adding nearly 300
      <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-111_Aardvark#FB-111A.2FF-111G" title="General Dynamics F-111 Aardvark">
       FB-111s
      </a>
      for shorter range roles then being filled by the
      <a href="/wiki/Convair_B-58_Hustler" title="Convair B-58 Hustler">
       B-58
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.119_11-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.119-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.576-8_28-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.576-8-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He again vetoed funding for AMSA aircraft development in 1968.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p23-6_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p23-6-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="B-1A_program">
       B-1A program
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: B-1A program">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RARF_Antenna,_Reflecting_Array_Radio_Frequency,_Raytheon,_1968-1969,_Ku-band,_over_3500_phase_shifting_modules,_for_the_AN-APQ-140_radar_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00376.JPG">

       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RARF_Antenna,_Reflecting_Array_Radio_Frequency,_Raytheon,_1968-1969,_Ku-band,_over_3500_phase_shifting_modules,_for_the_AN-APQ-140_radar_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00376.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=AN/APQ-140&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="AN/APQ-140 (page does not exist)">
         AN/APQ-140
        </a>
        radar for the B-1A
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          29
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      President
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">
       Richard Nixon
      </a>
      reestablished the AMSA program after taking office, keeping with his administration's
      <a href="/wiki/Flexible_response" title="Flexible response">
       flexible response
      </a>
      strategy that required a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_Integrated_Operations_Plan#Counterforce_migrates_to_deterrence_and_warfighting" title="Single Integrated Operations Plan">
       broad range of options short of general nuclear war
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.579_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.579-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nixon's Secretary of Defense,
      <a href="/wiki/Melvin_Laird" title="Melvin Laird">
       Melvin Laird
      </a>
      , reviewed the programs and decided to lower the numbers of FB-111s, since they lacked the desired range, and recommended that the AMSA design studies be accelerated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.579_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.579-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In April 1969, the program officially became the
      <i>
       B-1A
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.119_11-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.119-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.579_31-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.579-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was the first entry in the
      <a href="/wiki/1962_United_States_Tri-Service_aircraft_designation_system" title="1962 United States Tri-Service aircraft designation system">
       new bomber designation series
      </a>
      , first created in 1962. The Air Force issued a request for proposals in November 1969.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pace_p22-3_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pace_p22-3-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-1A_underside_1982.jpg">
        <img alt=" A B-1A flying with its wings swept back, showing its underside" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1848" data-file-width="2782" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/B-1A_underside_1982.jpg/220px-B-1A_underside_1982.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/B-1A_underside_1982.jpg/330px-B-1A_underside_1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/B-1A_underside_1982.jpg/440px-B-1A_underside_1982.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-1A_underside_1982.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        B-1A Prototype 4 showing its underside in 1981
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG">
        <img alt=" The nose section of a B-1A on display with outline of the ejection capsule denoted" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG/220px-B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG/330px-B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG/440px-B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B1A160wingsmuseum.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        B-1A nose section with ejection capsule denoted. Three of the four B-1As were fitted with escape capsules.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Proposals were submitted by Boeing, General Dynamics and North American Rockwell in January 1970.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pace_p22-3_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pace_p22-3-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 1970, North American Rockwell's design was selected and was awarded a development contract.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pace_p22-3_32-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pace_p22-3-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The original program called for two test airframes, five flyable aircraft, and 40 engines. This was cut in 1971 to one ground and three flight test aircraft.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.584_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.584-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The company changed its name to
      <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_International" title="Rockwell International">
       Rockwell International
      </a>
      and named its aircraft division North American Aircraft Operations in 1973.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A fourth prototype, built to production standards, was ordered in the fiscal year 1976 budget. Plans called for 240 B-1As to be built, with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Initial_operational_capability" title="Initial operational capability">
       initial operational capability
      </a>
      set for 1979.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrels_p.27_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sorrels_p.27-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Rockwell's design featured a number of features common to 1960s U.S. designs. Among these was the use of a "crew capsule" that ejected as a unit during emergencies, which was introduced to improve survivability in the case of an ejection at high speed. Additionally, the design featured large variable-sweep wings in order to provide both high
      <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">
       lift
      </a>
      during takeoff and landing, and low drag during a high-speed dash phase.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the wings set to their widest position the aircraft had considerably better lift and power than the B-52, allowing it to operate from a much wider variety of bases. Penetration of the
      <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
       Soviet Union
      </a>
      's defenses would take place at
      <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_speed" title="Supersonic speed">
       supersonic speed
      </a>
      , crossing them as quickly as possible before entering into the less defended "heartland" where speeds could be reduced again.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The large size and fuel capacity of the design would allow the "dash" portion of the flight to be relatively long.
     </p>
     <p>
      In order to achieve the required Mach2 performance at high altitudes, the exhaust nozzles and air
      <a href="/wiki/Intake_ramp" title="Intake ramp">
       intake ramps
      </a>
      were variable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Initially, it had been expected that a Mach1.2 performance could be achieved at low altitude, which required that titanium be used in critical areas in the fuselage and wing structure. The low altitude performance requirement was later lowered to Mach0.85, reducing the amount of titanium and therefore cost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.584_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.584-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A pair of small
      <a href="/wiki/Canard_(aeronautics)" title="Canard (aeronautics)">
       vanes
      </a>
      mounted near the nose are part of an active vibration damping system that smooths out the otherwise bumpy low-altitude ride.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first three B-1As featured an
      <a href="/wiki/Escape_crew_capsule" title="Escape crew capsule">
       escape capsule
      </a>
      that ejected the cockpit with all four crew members inside. The fourth B-1A was equipped with a conventional
      <a href="/wiki/Ejection_seat" title="Ejection seat">
       ejection seat
      </a>
      for each crew member.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1A mockup review occurred in late October 1971.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.586_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.586-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first B-1A prototype (Air Force serial no. 74-0158) flew on 23 December 1974. Three more B-1A prototypes followed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1B_backgrounder_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1B_backgrounder-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the program continued the per-unit cost continued to rise in part because of high
      <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
       inflation
      </a>
      during that period. In 1970, the estimated unit cost was $40 million, and by 1975, this figure had climbed to $70 million.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_problems_and_cancellation">
       New problems and cancellation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: New problems and cancellation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1976, Soviet pilot
      <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Belenko" title="Viktor Belenko">
       Viktor Belenko
      </a>
      defected to Japan with his
      <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-25" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-25">
       MiG-25 "Foxbat"
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During debriefing he described a new "super-Foxbat" (almost certainly referring to the
      <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan_MiG-31" title="Mikoyan MiG-31">
       MiG-31
      </a>
      ) that had
      <a href="/wiki/Look-down/shoot-down" title="Look-down/shoot-down">
       look-down/shoot-down
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">
       radar
      </a>
      in order to attack cruise missiles. This would also make any low-level penetration aircraft "visible" and easy to attack.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Given that the B-1's armament suite was similar to the B-52, and it now appeared no more likely to survive Soviet airspace than the B-52, the program was increasingly questioned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knaack_p.590_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Knaack_p.590-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In particular, Senator
      <a href="/wiki/William_Proxmire" title="William Proxmire">
       William Proxmire
      </a>
      continually derided it in public, arguing it was an outlandishly expensive dinosaur. During the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1976" title="United States presidential election, 1976">
       1976 federal election
      </a>
      campaign,
      <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">
       Jimmy Carter
      </a>
      made it one of the Democratic Party's platforms, saying "The B-1 bomber is an example of a proposed system which should not be funded and would be wasteful of taxpayers' dollars."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-time-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Carter took office in 1977 he ordered a review of the entire program. By this point the projected cost of the program had risen to over $100 million per aircraft, although this was lifetime cost over 20 years. He was informed of the relatively new work on
      <a href="/wiki/Stealth_aircraft" title="Stealth aircraft">
       stealth aircraft
      </a>
      that had started in 1975, and he decided that this was a better avenue of approach than the B-1.
      <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">
       Pentagon
      </a>
      officials also stated that the
      <a href="/wiki/AGM-86_ALCM" title="AGM-86 ALCM">
       AGM-86
      </a>
      Air Launched Cruise Missile (ALCM) launched from the existing B-52 fleet would give the USAF equal capability of penetrating Soviet airspace. With a range of 1,500 miles (2,400km), the ALCM could be launched well outside the range of any Soviet defenses and penetrate at low altitude just like a bomber (and much lower
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_cross-section" title="Radar cross-section">
       radar cross-section
      </a>
      due to size), but in much greater numbers at a lower cost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p.7_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p.7-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small number of B-52s could launch hundreds of ALCMs, saturating the defense. A program to improve the B-52 and develop and deploy the ALCM would cost perhaps 20% of the price to deploy the planned 244 B-1As.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-time-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 30 June 1977, Carter announced that the B-1A would be canceled in favor of ICBMs, SLBMs, and a fleet of modernized B-52s armed with ALCMs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrels_p.27_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sorrels_p.27-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Carter called it "one of the most difficult decisions that I've made since I've been in office." No mention of the stealth work was made public with the program being
      <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">
       top secret
      </a>
      , but today it is known that in early 1978 he authorized the
      <i>
       Advanced Technology Bomber
      </i>
      (ATB) project, which eventually led to the
      <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_B-2_Spirit" title="Northrop Grumman B-2 Spirit">
       B-2 Spirit
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pace_p20-27_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pace_p20-27-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Domestically, the reaction to the cancellation was split along partisan lines. The Department of Defense was surprised by the announcement; internal expectations were that the number of B-1s ordered would be reduced to around 150.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrels_p.23_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sorrels_p.23-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Congressman
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Dornan" title="Robert Dornan">
       Robert Dornan
      </a>
      (R-CA) claimed, "They're breaking out the vodka and caviar in Moscow."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast, it appears the Soviets were more concerned by large numbers of ALCMs representing a much greater threat than a smaller number of B-1s. Soviet news agency
      <a href="/wiki/Telegraph_Agency_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Telegraph Agency of the Soviet Union">
       TASS
      </a>
      commented that "the implementation of these militaristic plans has seriously complicated efforts for the limitation of the strategic arms race."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-time-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Western military leaders were generally happy with the decision. NATO commander
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Haig" title="Alexander Haig">
       Alexander Haig
      </a>
      described the ALCM as an "attractive alternative" to the B-1. French General Georges Buis stated "The B-1 is a formidable weapon, but not terribly useful. For the price of one bomber, you can have 200 cruise missiles."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_47-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-time-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Flight tests of the four B-1A prototypes for the B-1A program continued through April 1981. The program included 70 flights totaling 378 hours. A top speed of Mach2.22 was reached by the second B-1A. Engine testing also continued during this time with the YF101 engines totaling almost 7,600 hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p46_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p46-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Shifting_priorities">
       Shifting priorities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Shifting priorities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG">
        <img alt=" A right side view of a B-1A on the ground in 1984" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="657" data-file-width="2972" height="69" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG/310px-USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG/465px-USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG/620px-USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG 2x" width="310"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USAF_B-1A_Lancer.JPEG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A Rockwell B-1A in 1984
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      It was during this period that the Soviets started to assert themselves in several new theaters of action, in particular through
      <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
       Cuba
      </a>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Civil_War" title="Angolan Civil War">
       Angolan Civil War
      </a>
      starting in 1975 and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_war_in_Afghanistan" title="Soviet war in Afghanistan">
       Soviet invasion of Afghanistan
      </a>
      in 1979. U.S. strategy to this point had been focused on
      <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">
       containing
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">
       Communism
      </a>
      and preparation for war in Europe. The new Soviet actions revealed that the military lacked capability outside these narrow confines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LRCA_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LRCA-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The U.S. Army responded by accelerating its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Rapid_Deployment_Forces" title="United States Rapid Deployment Forces">
       Rapid Deployment Forces
      </a>
      concept but suffered from major problems with airlift and sealift capability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In order to slow an enemy invasion of other countries, air power was critical; however the key Iran-Afghanistan border was outside the range of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy">
       U.S. Navy
      </a>
      's carrier-based attack aircraft, leaving this role to the air force. Although the B-52 had the range to support on-demand global missions, its long runway requirements limited the forward basing possibilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 1980 presidential campaign,
      <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">
       Ronald Reagan
      </a>
      campaigned heavily on the platform that Carter was weak on defense, citing the cancellation of the B-1 program as an example, a theme he continued using into the 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During this time Carter's defense secretary,
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Brown_(Secretary_of_Defense)" title="Harold Brown (Secretary of Defense)">
       Harold Brown
      </a>
      , announced the stealth bomber project, apparently implying that this was the reason for the B-1 cancellation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.120_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.120-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="B-1B_program">
       B-1B program
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: B-1B program">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On taking office, Reagan was faced with the same decision as Carter before: whether to continue with the B-1 for the short term, or to wait for the development of the ATB, a much more advanced aircraft. Studies suggested that the existing B-52 fleet with ALCM would remain a credible threat until 1985, as it was predicted that 75% of the B-52 force would survive to attack its targets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post-b-52_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-post-b-52-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After this, the introduction of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SA-10" title="SA-10">
       SA-10
      </a>
      missile, the MiG-31 interceptor and the first Soviet
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airborne_Early_Warning_and_Control" title="Airborne Early Warning and Control">
       Airborne Early Warning and Control
      </a>
      (AWACS) systems would make the B-52 increasingly vulnerable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During 1981, funds were allocated to a new study for a bomber for the 1990s time-frame, this led to the
      <i>
       Long-Range Combat Aircraft
      </i>
      (LRCA) project. The LRCA evaluated the B-1, F-111 and ATB as possible solutions; an emphasis was placed on multi-role capabilities, as opposed to purely strategic operations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post-b-52_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-post-b-52-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_B-1,_Palmdale.jpg">
        <img alt=" The first B-1B at its roll-out ceremony outside a hangar in Palmdale, California in 1984" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="690" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg/220px-First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg/330px-First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f6/First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg/440px-First_B-1%2C_Palmdale.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_B-1,_Palmdale.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        First B-1B debuted outside a hangar in
        <a href="/wiki/Palmdale,_California" title="Palmdale, California">
         Palmdale, California
        </a>
        , 1984
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1981, it was believed the B-1 could be in operation before the ATB, covering the transitionary period between the B-52's increasing vulnerability and the ATB's introduction. Reagan decided the best solution was to procure both the B-1 and ATB, and on 2 October 1981 Reagan announced that 100 B-1s were to be ordered to fill the LRCA role.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reagan_approves_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reagan_approves-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In January 1982 the U.S. Air Force awarded two contracts to Rockwell worth a combined $2.2 billion for the development and production of 100 new B-1 bombers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p62_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p62-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Numerous changes were made to the design to make it better suited to the now expected missions, resulting in the new
      <i>
       B-1B
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p.7_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p.7-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These changes included a reduction in maximum speed,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.120_57-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.120-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which allowed the variable-aspect
      <a href="/wiki/Inlet_cone" title="Inlet cone">
       intake ramps
      </a>
      to be replaced by simpler fixed geometry intake ramps in the newer design. This reduced the B version's radar signature; the reduction in
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_cross-section" title="Radar cross-section">
       radar cross-section
      </a>
      was seen as a good trade off for the speed decrease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      High subsonic speeds at low altitude became a focus area for the revised design,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_p.120_57-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartz_p.120-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and low-level speeds were increased from about Mach0.85 to 0.92. The B-1B has a maximum speed of Mach1.25 at higher altitudes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNA_B-1B_specs_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BNA_B-1B_specs-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1B's maximum takeoff weight was increased to 477,000 pounds (216,000kg) from the B-1A's 395,000 pounds (179,000kg).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p28_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p28-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The weight increase was to allow for takeoff with a full internal fuel load and for external weapons to be carried. Rockwell engineers were able to reinforce critical areas and lighten non-critical areas of the airframe, so the increase in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_aircraft_empty_weight" title="Basic aircraft empty weight">
       empty weight
      </a>
      was minimal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p28_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p28-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In order to deal with the introduction of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MiG-31" title="MiG-31">
       MiG-31
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Zaslon" title="Zaslon">
       Zaslon
      </a>
      ) and other aircraft with
      <a href="/wiki/Look-down/shoot-down" title="Look-down/shoot-down">
       look-down
      </a>
      capability (which reduced the B-1s low-flying advantage), the B-1B's
      <a href="/wiki/Electronic_warfare" title="Electronic warfare">
       electronic warfare
      </a>
      suite was significantly upgraded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-1_wings_swept.jpg">
        <img alt=" B-1B with its wings swept back doing a banked turn during a demonstration" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/B-1_wings_swept.jpg/220px-B-1_wings_swept.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/B-1_wings_swept.jpg/330px-B-1_wings_swept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/B-1_wings_swept.jpg/440px-B-1_wings_swept.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-1_wings_swept.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        B-1B banking during a demonstration in 2004
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Opposition to the plan was widespread within Congress. Critics pointed out that many of the original problems remained in both areas of performance and expense.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In particular it seemed the B-52 fitted with electronics similar to the B-1B would be equally able to avoid interception, as the speed advantage of the B-1 was now minimal. It also appeared that the "interim" time frame served by the B-1B would be less than a decade, being rendered obsolete shortly after the introduction of a much more capable ATB design.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The primary argument in favor of the B-1 was its large
      <a href="/wiki/Conventional_weapon" title="Conventional weapon">
       conventional weapon
      </a>
      payload, and that its takeoff performance allowed it to operate with a credible bombload from a much wider variety of airfields. The air force spread production subcontracts across many congressional districts, making the aircraft more popular on
      <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Hill" title="Capitol Hill">
       Capitol Hill
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-post-b-52_58-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-post-b-52-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      B-1A #1 was disassembled and used for radar testing at the
      <a href="/wiki/Rome_Laboratory" title="Rome Laboratory">
       Rome Air Development Center
      </a>
      at the former
      <a href="/wiki/Griffiss_Air_Force_Base" title="Griffiss Air Force Base">
       Griffiss Air Force Base
      </a>
      , New York.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p70_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p70-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      B-1As #2 and #4 were modified to include B-1B systems. The first B-1B was completed and began flight testing in March 1983. The first production B-1B was rolled out on 4 September 1984 and first flew on 18 October 1984.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p63-4_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p63-4-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 100th and final B-1B was delivered on 2 May 1988;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1B_backgrounder_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1B_backgrounder-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      before the last B-1B was delivered, the air force had determined that the aircraft was vulnerable to Soviet air defenses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_1_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_1-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Design">
       Design
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Design">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
       Overview
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Overview">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The B-1 has a
      <a href="/wiki/Blended_wing_body" title="Blended wing body">
       blended wing body
      </a>
      configuration, with
      <a href="/wiki/Variable-sweep_wing" title="Variable-sweep wing">
       variable-sweep wing
      </a>
      , four
      <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">
       turbofan engines
      </a>
      , triangular fin control surfaces and
      <a href="/wiki/Cruciform_tail" title="Cruciform tail">
       cruciform tail
      </a>
      . The wings can sweep from 15 degrees to 67.5 degrees (full forward to full sweep). Forward-swept wing settings are used for
      <a href="/wiki/Takeoff" title="Takeoff">
       takeoff
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">
       landings
      </a>
      and high-altitude maximum
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cruise_(flight)" title="Cruise (flight)">
       cruise
      </a>
      . Aft-swept wing settings are used in high subsonic and supersonic flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1's variable-sweep wings and
      <a href="/wiki/Thrust-to-weight_ratio" title="Thrust-to-weight ratio">
       thrust-to-weight ratio
      </a>
      provide it with better takeoff performance, allowing it to use more runways than previous bombers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The length of the aircraft presented a flexing problem due to air turbulence at low altitude. To alleviate this, Rockwell included small triangular fin control surfaces or vanes near the nose on the B-1. The B-1's Structural Mode Control System rotates the vanes automatically to counteract turbulence and smooth out the ride.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAT2004-B1B.jpg">
        <img alt=" A rear view of a B-1B at Royal International Air Tattoo air show in 2004" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="521" data-file-width="1024" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RIAT2004-B1B.jpg/220px-RIAT2004-B1B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RIAT2004-B1B.jpg/330px-RIAT2004-B1B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/RIAT2004-B1B.jpg/440px-RIAT2004-B1B.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAT2004-B1B.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Rear view of B-1B in flight, 2004
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Unlike the B-1A, the B-1B cannot reach Mach2+ speeds; its maximum speed is Mach1.25 (about 950mph or 1,530km/h at altitude),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p60_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p60-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but its low-level speed increased to Mach0.92 (700mph, 1,130km/h).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BNA_B-1B_specs_62-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BNA_B-1B_specs-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The speed of the current version of the aircraft is limited by the need to avoid damage to its structure and air intakes. To help lower its radar cross section (RCS), the B-1B uses
      <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_shape" title="Serpentine shape">
       serpentine
      </a>
      air
      <a href="/wiki/Intake" title="Intake">
       intake ducts
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/S-duct" title="S-duct">
       S-duct
      </a>
      ) and fixed
      <a href="/wiki/Intake_ramp" title="Intake ramp">
       intake ramps
      </a>
      , which limit its speed compared to the B-1A. Vanes in the intake ducts serve to deflect and shield radar emissions from the highly reflective engine compressor blades.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p44-5_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p44-5-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1A's engine was modified slightly to produce the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_F101" title="General Electric F101">
       GE F101-102
      </a>
      for the B-1B, with an emphasis on durability, and increased efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Modern_Warplanes_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Modern_Warplanes-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The core of this engine has since been re-used in several other engine designs, including the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_F110" title="General Electric F110">
       GE F110
      </a>
      which has seen use in the F-14 Tomcat,
      <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-15E_Strike_Eagle" title="McDonnell Douglas F-15E Strike Eagle">
       F-15K/SG
      </a>
      variants and most recent versions of the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon">
       General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GE_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GE-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is also the basis for the non-
      <a href="/wiki/Afterburner" title="Afterburner">
       afterburning
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_F118" title="General Electric F118">
       GE F118
      </a>
      used in the B-2 Spirit and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">
       U-2S
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GE_75-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GE-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The F101 engine was the basis for the core of the extremely popular
      <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_CFM56" title="CFM International CFM56">
       CFM56
      </a>
      civil engine, which can be found on some versions of practically every small-to-medium-sized airliner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The nose gear cover door has controls for the
      <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">
       auxiliary power units
      </a>
      (APUs), which allow for quick starts of the APUs upon order to scramble.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p44_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p44-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pace_p44_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pace_p44-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Avionics">
       Avionics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Avionics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B-1cockpitnight.jpg">
        <img alt="The interior of a B-1B cockpit at night" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="560" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/B-1cockpitnight.jpg/220px-B-1cockpitnight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/B-1cockpitnight.jpg/330px-B-1cockpitnight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/B-1cockpitnight.jpg/440px-B-1cockpitnight.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B-1cockpitnight.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        B-1B cockpit at night
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AN-APQ-164_radar,_Westinghouse,_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4038" data-file-width="3269" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG/220px-AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG/330px-AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG/440px-AN-APQ-164_radar%2C_Westinghouse%2C_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AN-APQ-164_radar,_Westinghouse,_developed_in_1982_-_National_Electronics_Museum_-_DSC00384.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        AN/APQ-164
        <a href="/wiki/Passive_electronically_scanned_array" title="Passive electronically scanned array">
         Passive electronically scanned array
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The B-1's main computer is the
      <a href="/wiki/IBM_AP-101" title="IBM AP-101">
       IBM AP-101
      </a>
      , which is also used on the
      <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_orbiter" title="Space Shuttle orbiter">
       Space Shuttle orbiter
      </a>
      and the B-52 bomber.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap101_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ap101-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The computer is programmed with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JOVIAL_(programming_language)" title="JOVIAL (programming language)">
       JOVIAL
      </a>
      programming language.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jovial_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jovial-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Lancer's offensive
      <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">
       avionics
      </a>
      include the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electronic_Systems" title="Westinghouse Electronic Systems">
       Westinghouse
      </a>
      (now
      <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman" title="Northrop Grumman">
       Northrop Grumman
      </a>
      )
      <span id="AN.2FAPQ-164">
      </span>
      <span id="APQ-164">
      </span>
      <span id="radar">
      </span>
      AN/APQ-164 forward-looking offensive
      <a href="/wiki/Passive_electronically_scanned_array" title="Passive electronically scanned array">
       passive electronically scanned array
      </a>
      radar set with electronic beam steering (and a fixed antenna pointed downward for reduced radar observability),
      <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">
       synthetic aperture radar
      </a>
      , ground
      <a href="/wiki/Moving_target_indication" title="Moving target indication">
       moving target indication
      </a>
      (GMTI), and
