      <a href="/wiki/Terrain-following_radar" title="Terrain-following radar">
       terrain-following radar
      </a>
      modes,
      <a href="/wiki/Doppler_radar#Doppler_navigation" title="Doppler radar">
       Doppler navigation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_altimeter" title="Radar altimeter">
       radar altimeter
      </a>
      , and an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_navigation" title="Inertial navigation">
       inertial navigation
      </a>
      suite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1B Block D upgrade added a
      <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">
       Global Positioning System
      </a>
      (GPS) receiver beginning in 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p33-4_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p33-4-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1's defensive electronics include the
      <a href="/wiki/Eaton_Corporation" title="Eaton Corporation">
       Eaton
      </a>
      AN/ALQ-161A
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_warning_receiver" title="Radar warning receiver">
       radar warning
      </a>
      and defensive
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_jamming_and_deception" title="Radar jamming and deception">
       jamming
      </a>
      equipment,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.15_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.15-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which has three sets of antennas; one at the front base of each wing and the third rear-facing in the tail
      <a href="/wiki/Radome" title="Radome">
       radome
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_1986_p52-3_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spick_1986_p52-3-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p106_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p106-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also in the tail radome is the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=AN/ALQ-153&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="AN/ALQ-153 (page does not exist)">
       AN/ALQ-153
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Missile_Approach_Warning" title="Missile Approach Warning">
       Missile Approach Warning
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">
       Pulse-Doppler radar
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ALQ-161 is linked to a total of eight AN/ALE-49
      <a href="/wiki/Flare_(countermeasure)" title="Flare (countermeasure)">
       flare
      </a>
      dispensers located on top behind the canopy, which are handled by the AN/ASQ-184 avionics management system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skaarup_p.18_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Skaarup_p.18-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each AN/ALE-49 dispenser has a capacity of 12 MJU-23A/B flares. The MJU-23A/B flare is one of the world's largest
      <a href="/wiki/Infrared_countermeasure" title="Infrared countermeasure">
       infrared countermeasure
      </a>
      flares at a weight of over 3.3 pounds (1.5kg).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1 has also been equipped to carry the
      <a href="/wiki/ALE-50_Towed_Decoy_System" title="ALE-50 Towed Decoy System">
       ALE-50 Towed Decoy System
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_sheet_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_sheet-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Also aiding the B-1's survivability is its relatively low
      <a href="/wiki/Radar_cross-section" title="Radar cross-section">
       radar cross-section
      </a>
      (RCS). Although not technically a
      <a href="/wiki/Stealth_aircraft" title="Stealth aircraft">
       stealth aircraft
      </a>
      in a comprehensive sense, thanks to the aircraft's structure, serpentine intake paths and use of
      <a href="/wiki/Radar-absorbent_material" title="Radar-absorbent material">
       radar-absorbent material
      </a>
      its RCS is about 1/50th of the similar sized B-52's RCS; this is about 26ft or 2.4m, roughly equivalent to the RCS of a small
      <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">
       fighter aircraft
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skaarup_p.18_87-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Skaarup_p.18-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Upgrades">
       Upgrades
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Upgrades">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg">
        <img alt="Sideview of a B-1B's nose section, which features a Sniper XR pod mounted on its chin" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="241" data-file-width="600" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg/220px-A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg/330px-A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg/440px-A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_B-1B_Lancer_with_a_Sniper_pod.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Nose of B-1 with the
        <a href="/wiki/Sniper_Advanced_Targeting_Pod" title="Sniper Advanced Targeting Pod">
         Sniper XR pod
        </a>
        hanging below
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The B-1 has been upgraded since production, beginning with the "Conventional Mission Upgrade Program" (CMUP), which added a new
      <a href="/wiki/MIL-STD-1760" title="MIL-STD-1760">
       MIL-STD-1760
      </a>
      smart-weapons interface to enable the use of precision-guided conventional weapons. CMUP began with Block A, which was the standard B-1B with the capability to deliver non-precision gravity bombs. Block B brought an improved Synthetic Aperture Radar, and upgrades to the Defensive Countermeasures System and was fielded in 1995. Block C provided an "enhanced capability" for delivery of up to 30 cluster bomb units (CBUs) per
      <a href="/wiki/Sortie" title="Sortie">
       sortie
      </a>
      with modifications made to 50 bomb racks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skaarup_p.19_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Skaarup_p.19-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Block D added a "Near Precision Capability" via improved weapons and targeting systems, and added advanced secure communications capabilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skaarup_p.19_92-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Skaarup_p.19-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first part of the electronic countermeasures upgrade added
      <a href="/wiki/Joint_Direct_Attack_Munition" title="Joint Direct Attack Munition">
       Joint Direct Attack Munition
      </a>
      (JDAM),
      <a href="/wiki/ALE-50_Towed_Decoy_System" title="ALE-50 Towed Decoy System">
       ALE-50 Towed Decoy System
      </a>
      , and anti-jam radios.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.15_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.15-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Block E upgraded the avionics computers and incorporated the
      <a href="/wiki/Wind_Corrected_Munitions_Dispenser" title="Wind Corrected Munitions Dispenser">
       Wind Corrected Munitions Dispenser
      </a>
      (WCMD), the
      <a href="/wiki/AGM-154_Joint_Standoff_Weapon" title="AGM-154 Joint Standoff Weapon">
       AGM-154 Joint Standoff Weapon
      </a>
      (JSOW) and the
      <a href="/wiki/AGM-158_JASSM" title="AGM-158 JASSM">
       AGM-158 JASSM
      </a>
      (Joint Air to Surface Standoff Munition), substantially improving the bomber's capability. Upgrades were completed in September 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Block F was the Defensive Systems Upgrade Program (DSUP) to improve the aircraft's electronic countermeasures and jamming capabilities, but it was canceled in December 2002 due to cost overruns and schedule slips.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2005, a program began to upgrade crew stations and integrate data linking.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A B-1 equipped with the Fully Integrated Data Link (FIDL) first flew on 29 July 2009; the FIDL enables electronic data sharing, eliminating the need to enter information between systems by hand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In January 2013, Boeing delivered the first Integrated Battle Station (IBS) equipped B-1. This replaced several displays with new multi-function color display units, an upgraded Central Integrated Test System, and a newer Aircraft Performance Monitoring Computer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1B_Upgrade_Contract_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1B_Upgrade_Contract-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2012, the B-1Bs are receiving Sustainment-Block 16 upgrades to add
      <a href="/wiki/Link_16" title="Link 16">
       Link 16
      </a>
      networking and digital flight instrumentation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2007 the
      <a href="/wiki/Sniper_Advanced_Targeting_Pod" title="Sniper Advanced Targeting Pod">
       Sniper XR
      </a>
      targeting pod was integrated on the B-1 fleet. The pod is mounted on an external hardpoint at the aircraft's chin near the forward bomb bay.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following accelerated testing, the Sniper pod was fielded in summer 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sniper_combat_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sniper_combat-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Future precision munitions include the
      <a href="/wiki/Small_Diameter_Bomb" title="Small Diameter Bomb">
       Small Diameter Bomb
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011, the Air Force was considering upgrading the B-1s with multiple ejector racks so that they can carry three times as many smaller JDAMs than they currently can.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In February 2014, work began on a multi-year upgrade of 62 B-1Bs, scheduled to be completed by 2019. The vertical situation display upgrade (VDSU) shall replace existing flight instruments with multifunction color displays, a second display shall aid threat evasion and targeting, and act as a back-up display. Additional memory capacity is to be installed for the diagnostics database. Procurement and installation of the IBS upgrades is expected to cost $918 million, research and engineering costs are estimated at $391 million. Other additions are to replace the two spinning mass gyroscopic inertial navigation system with
      <a href="/wiki/Ring_laser_gyroscope" title="Ring laser gyroscope">
       ring laser gyroscopic
      </a>
      systems and a GPS antenna, replacement of the
      <a href="/wiki/AN/APG-66" title="AN/APG-66">
       APQ-164 radar
      </a>
      , and a new attitude indicator.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defensetech21feb14_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-defensetech21feb14-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">
       Operational history
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Operational history">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Strategic_Air_Command">
       Strategic Air Command
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Strategic Air Command">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The second B-1B, "The Star of Abilene", was the first B-1B delivered to the USAF
      <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">
       Strategic Air Command
      </a>
      (SAC) in June 1985. Initial operational capability was reached on 1 October 1986 and the B-1B was placed on nuclear alert status.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p83_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p83-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1 received the official name "Lancer" on 15 March 1990. However, the bomber has been commonly called the "Bone"; a nickname that appears to stem from an early newspaper article on the aircraft wherein its name was phonetically spelled out as "B-ONE" with the hyphen inadvertently omitted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p67_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p67-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_(6327003326).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg/220px-Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg/330px-Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg/440px-Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_%286327003326%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Decommissioned_B-1_Bomber_Travels_Along_I-5_(6327003326).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A dismantled decommissioned B-1 being transported by flatbed truck
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In late 1990 engine fires in two Lancers caused the grounding of the fleet. The cause was traced back to problems in the first-stage fan, the aircraft were placed on "limited alert"; in other words, they were grounded unless a nuclear war broke out. Following inspections and repairs they were returned to duty beginning on 6 February 1991.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p116_112-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p116-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to the engine problems, the B-1B was effectively sidelined in the
      <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">
       Gulf War
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_1_68-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_1-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Originally designed strictly for nuclear war, the B-1's development as an effective conventional bomber was delayed until the 1990s. The collapse of the Soviet Union had brought the B-1's nuclear role into question, leading to President
      <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">
       George H. W. Bush
      </a>
      ordering a $3 billion conventional refit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_4_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_4-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1991, the B-1 had a fledgling conventional capability, forty of them able to drop the 500 pounds (230kg) Mk-82 General Purpose (GP) bomb, although mostly from low altitude. Despite being cleared for this role, the problems with the engines precluded their use in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Desert_Storm" title="Operation Desert Storm">
       Operation Desert Storm
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p._10_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p._10-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      B-1s were primarily reserved for strategic nuclear strike missions at this time, providing the role of airborne nuclear deterrent against the Soviet Union.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p._10_115-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p._10-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-52 was more suited to the role of conventional warfare and it was used by coalition forces instead.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p._10_115-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p._10-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the inactivation of Strategic Air Command (SAC) and the establishment of the
      <a href="/wiki/Air_Combat_Command" title="Air Combat Command">
       Air Combat Command
      </a>
      (ACC) in 1992, the B-1 developed a greater conventional weapons capability. Part of this development was the start-up of the U.S. Air Force Weapons School B-1 Division.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1994, two additional B-1 bomb wings were also created in the
      <a href="/wiki/Air_National_Guard" title="Air National Guard">
       Air National Guard
      </a>
      , with former fighter wings in the
      <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Air_National_Guard" title="Kansas Air National Guard">
       Kansas Air National Guard
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Air_National_Guard" title="Georgia Air National Guard">
       Georgia Air National Guard
      </a>
      converting to the aircraft.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Withington_p._11_117-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Withington_p._11-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the mid-1990s, the B-1 could employ GP weapons as well as various CBUs. By the end of the 1990s, with the advent of the "Block D" upgrade, the B-1 boasted a full array of guided and unguided munitions. The B-1B no longer carries nuclear weapons;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      its nuclear capability was disabled by 1995 with the removal of nuclear arming and fuzing hardware.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p141_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p141-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_role">
       Conventional role
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Conventional role">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B1s.jpg">
        <img alt="Top forward view of gray aircraft with wings swept forward banking right. Underneath are strips of white clouds and uninhabited terrain." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1219" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/B1s.jpg/220px-B1s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/B1s.jpg/330px-B1s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/B1s.jpg/440px-B1s.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B1s.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A B-1B Lancer with wings swept full forward
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The B-1 was first used in combat in support of operations against
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      , during
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Desert_Fox" title="Operation Desert Fox">
       Operation Desert Fox
      </a>
      in December 1998, employing unguided GP weapons. B-1s have been subsequently used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Allied_Force" title="Operation Allied Force">
       Operation Allied Force
      </a>
      (Kosovo) and, most notably, in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001-present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001-present)">
       Operation Enduring Freedom
      </a>
      in Afghanistan and the
      <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">
       2003 invasion of Iraq
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1's role in Operation Allied Force has been criticized as the aircraft was not used until after enemy defenses had been suppressed by aircraft like the older B-52 it was intended to replace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_1_68-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_1-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1 has deployed an array of conventional weapons in war zones, most notably the GBU-31, 2,000 pounds (910kg)
      <a href="/wiki/Joint_Direct_Attack_Munition" title="Joint Direct Attack Munition">
       Joint Direct Attack Munition
      </a>
      (JDAM).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_p.13_37-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee_p.13-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the first six months of Operation Enduring Freedom, eight B-1s dropped almost 40 percent of aerial ordnance, including some 3,900 JDAMs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defensetech21feb14_108-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-defensetech21feb14-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      JDAM munitions were heavily used by the B-1 over Iraq, notably on 7 April 2003 in an unsuccessful attempt to kill
      <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">
       Saddam Hussein
      </a>
      and his two sons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the height of the Iraq War, a B-1 was permanently airborne to provide rapid precision bombardment upon important targets as intelligence identified them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      During Operation Enduring Freedom, the B-1 was able to raise its
      <a href="/wiki/Availability" title="Availability">
       mission capable rate
      </a>
      to 79%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_sheet_89-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_sheet-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1 has higher survivability and speed when compared to the older B-52, which it was intended to replace. It also holds 61
      <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="Fdration Aronautique Internationale">
       FAI
      </a>
      world records for speed, payload, distance, and time-to-climb in different aircraft weight classes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_appE_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_appE-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In November 1983, three B-1Bs set a long distance record for the aircraft, which demonstrated its ability to conduct extended mission lengths to strike anywhere in the world and return to base without any stops.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/National_Aeronautic_Association" title="National Aeronautic Association">
       National Aeronautic Association
      </a>
      recognized the B-1B for completing one of the 10 most memorable record flights for 1994.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_sheet_89-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_sheet-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Of the 100 B-1Bs built, 93 remained in 2000 after losses in accidents. In June 2001, the Pentagon sought to place one-third of its then fleet into storage; this proposal resulted in several U.S. Air National Guard officers and members of Congress lobbying against the proposal, including the drafting of an amendment to prevent such cuts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_1_68-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_1-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 2001 proposal was intended to allow money to be diverted to further upgrades to the remaining B-1Bs, such as computer modernization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dao_1_68-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-dao_1-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2003, accompanied by the removal of B-1Bs from the two bomb wings in the Air National Guard, the USAF decided to retire 33 aircraft to concentrate its budget on maintaining availability of remaining B-1Bs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2004, a new
      <a href="/wiki/Appropriation_bill" title="Appropriation bill">
       appropriation bill
      </a>
      called for some of the retired aircraft to return to service,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the USAF returned seven mothballed bombers to service to increase the fleet to 67 aircraft.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2005_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2005-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg">
        <img alt="Ammunition and yellow bombs lay as two crew member, using lift truck, transfer them to a gray B-1 parked nearby in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="703" data-file-width="1050" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg/220px-JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg/330px-JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg/440px-JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JDAM_B-1B_Lancer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crew members transferring a
        <a href="/wiki/Joint_Direct_Attack_Munition" title="Joint Direct Attack Munition">
         GBU-31 Joint Direct Attack Munition
        </a>
        (JDAM) to a lift truck for loading onto a B-1B on 29 March 2007, in Southwest Asia
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 14 July 2007, the Associated Press reported on the growing USAF presence in Iraq, including reintroduction of B-1Bs as a close-at-hand platform to support Coalition ground forces.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_B-1_recall_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AP_B-1_recall-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since 2008, B-1s have been used in Iraq and Afghanistan in an "armed overwatch" role, loitering for surveillance purposes while ready to deliver guided bombs in support of ground troops if contacted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancer_Force_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lancer_Force-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The B-1B underwent a series of flight tests using a 50/50 mix of synthetic and petroleum fuel; on 19 March 2008, a B-1B from
      <a href="/wiki/Dyess_Air_Force_Base" title="Dyess Air Force Base">
       Dyess Air Force Base
      </a>
      , Texas, became the first USAF aircraft to fly at supersonic speed using a
      <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">
       synthetic fuel
      </a>
      during a flight over Texas and New Mexico. This was conducted as part of an ongoing Air Force testing and certification program to reduce reliance on traditional oil sources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 4 August 2008, a B-1B flew the first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_Sniper_XR" title="Lockheed Martin Sniper XR">
       Sniper Advanced Targeting Pod
      </a>
      equipped combat sortie where the crew successfully targeted enemy ground forces and dropped a GBU-38 guided bomb in Afghanistan.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sniper_combat_104-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sniper_combat-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In March 2011, B-1Bs from
      <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Air_Force_Base" title="Ellsworth Air Force Base">
       Ellsworth Air Force Base
      </a>
      attacked undisclosed targets in
      <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">
       Libya
      </a>
      as part of
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Odyssey_Dawn" title="Operation Odyssey Dawn">
       Operation Odyssey Dawn
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The USAF had 66 B-1Bs in service in September 2012, split between four squadrons organized into two Bomb Wings: the 7th Bomb Wing at
      <a href="/wiki/Dyess_Air_Force_Base" title="Dyess Air Force Base">
       Dyess AFB
      </a>
      , Texas, and the 28th Bomb Wing at
      <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Air_Force_Base" title="Ellsworth Air Force Base">
       Ellsworth AFB
      </a>
      , South Dakota.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1B_Upgrade_Contract_100-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1B_Upgrade_Contract-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2013_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2013-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With upgrades to keep the B-1 viable, the air force may keep it in service until approximately 2038.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite upgrades, the B-1 has repair and cost issues; every flight hour needs 48.4 hours of repair. The fuel, repairs and other needs for a 12-hour mission costs $720,000 as of 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The $63,000 cost per flight hour is, however, less than the $72,000 for the B-52 and the $135,000 of the B-2.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-axe20120326_135-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-axe20120326-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2010, senior USAF officials met to consider retiring the entire fleet to meet budget cuts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Pentagon plans to supplement the aircraft with the
      <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Strike_Bomber" title="Long Range Strike Bomber">
       Long Range Strike Bomber
      </a>
      beginning in 2030.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LRS-B_replace_138-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LRS-B_replace-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the meantime, its "capabilities are particularly well-suited to the vast distances and unique challenges of the Pacific region, and we'll continue to invest in, and rely on, the B-1 in support of the focus on the Pacific" as part of President Obama's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China%E2%80%93United_States_relations#Obama_administration" title="People's Republic of ChinaUnited States relations">
       "Pivot to East Asia"
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In August 2012, the 9th Expeditionary Bomb Squadron returned from a six-month tour in Afghanistan. Their nine B-1Bs flew 770 sorties, the most of any B-1B squadron on a single deployment. The squadron spent 9,500 hours airborne, having one of its bombers in the air at all times. They accounted for a quarter of all combat aircraft sorties over the country while there and averaged 23 requests for air support per day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 4 September 2013, a B-1B participated in a maritime evaluation exercise, deploying munitions such as laser-guided 500lb
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GBU-54" title="GBU-54">
       GBU-54
      </a>
      bombs, 500lb and 2,000lb
      <a href="/wiki/Joint_Direct_Attack_Munition" title="Joint Direct Attack Munition">
       Joint Direct Attack Munitions
      </a>
      (JDAM), and
      <a href="/wiki/Long_Range_Anti-Ship_Missile" title="Long Range Anti-Ship Missile">
       Long Range Anti-Ship Missiles
      </a>
      (LRASM). The aim was to detect and engage several small craft using existing weapons and tactics developed from conventional warfare against ground targets; the B-1 is seen as a useful asset for maritime duties such as patrolling shipping lanes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Beginning in 2014, the B-1 was used by the U.S. against
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIL" title="ISIL">
       ISIL
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">
       Syrian Civil War
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From August 2014 to January 2015, the B-1 accounted for eight percent of USAF sorties during
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Inherent_Resolve" title="Operation Inherent Resolve">
       Operation Inherent Resolve
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">
       Variants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Variants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B1ArearWingsMus.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/B1ArearWingsMus.JPG/220px-B1ArearWingsMus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/B1ArearWingsMus.JPG/330px-B1ArearWingsMus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/B1ArearWingsMus.JPG/440px-B1ArearWingsMus.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B1ArearWingsMus.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The rear section showing the B-1A's pointed radome
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       B-1A
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The B-1A was the original B-1 design with variable engine intakes and Mach2.2 top speed. Four prototypes were built; no production units were manufactured.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2005_126-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AF_Alma_facts-figs_2005-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complete_Encyclopedia_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Complete_Encyclopedia-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       B-1B
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The B-1B is a revised B-1 design with reduced radar signature and a top speed of Mach1.25. It was otherwise optimized for low-level penetration. A total of 100 B-1Bs were produced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complete_Encyclopedia_144-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Complete_Encyclopedia-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       B-1R
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The B-1R is a proposed upgrade of existing B-1B aircraft.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1R_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1R-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The B-1R (R for "regional") would be fitted with advanced radars,
      <a href="/wiki/Air-to-air_missile" title="Air-to-air missile">
       air-to-air missiles
      </a>
      , and new
      <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_F119" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney F119">
       Pratt &amp; Whitney F119
      </a>
      engines. This variant would have a top speed of Mach2.2, but with 20% shorter range.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hebert_Long-Range_Strike_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hebert_Long-Range_Strike-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Existing external hardpoints would be modified to allow multiple conventional weapons to be carried, increasing overall loadout. For air-to-air defense, an
      <a href="/wiki/Active_electronically_scanned_array" title="Active electronically scanned array">
       Active electronically scanned array
      </a>
      (AESA) radar would be added and some existing hardpoints modified to carry air-to-air missiles. If needed the B-1R could escape from unfavorable air-to-air encounters with its Mach2+ speed. Few aircraft are currently capable of sustained speeds over Mach2.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1R_145-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-B-1R-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_on_display">
       Aircraft on display
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Aircraft on display">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       B-1A
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       74-0160 -
       <a href="/wiki/Wings_Over_the_Rockies_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="Wings Over the Rockies Air and Space Museum">
        Wings Over the Rockies Museum
       </a>
       at the former
       <a href="/wiki/Lowry_Air_Force_Base" title="Lowry Air Force Base">
        Lowry Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denver,_Colorado" title="Denver, Colorado">
        Denver, Colorado
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         146
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       74-0174 -
       <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="Strategic Air and Space Museum">
        Strategic Air and Space Museum
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Ashland,_Nebraska" title="Ashland, Nebraska">
        Ashland, Nebraska
       </a>
       . This aircraft has conventional
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ejection_seats" title="Ejection seats">
        ejection seats
       </a>
       and other features used on the B-1B variant.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         147
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       B-1B
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <ul>
      <li>
       83-0065
       <i>
        Star of Abilene
       </i>
       - Dyess Linear Air Park at
       <a href="/wiki/Dyess_Air_Force_Base" title="Dyess Air Force Base">
        Dyess Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
        Texas
       </a>
       . This was the first aircraft delivered to the U.S. Air Force. Dyess AFB is home to one of two active Air Force B-1B wings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         148
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0066
       <i>
        Ole Puss
       </i>
       - Heritage Park at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_Home_AFB" title="Mountain Home AFB">
        Mountain Home AFB
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">
        Idaho
       </a>
       with wheels in the wells.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         149
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0067
       <i>
        Texas Raider
       </i>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="South Dakota Air and Space Museum">
        South Dakota Air and Space Museum
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Ellsworth_Air_Force_Base" title="Ellsworth Air Force Base">
        Ellsworth Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">
        South Dakota
       </a>
       . Ellsworth AFB is home to one of two active Air Force B-1B wings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
        <a href="#cite_note-151">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         150
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0068
       <i>
        Spuds
       </i>
       - Reflections of Freedom Air Park at
       <a href="/wiki/McConnell_Air_Force_Base" title="McConnell Air Force Base">
        McConnell Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">
        Wichita, Kansas
       </a>
       , a former Air Force and
       <a href="/wiki/Air_National_Guard" title="Air National Guard">
        Air National Guard
       </a>
       B-1B base.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         151
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0069
       <i>
        Silent Penetrator
       </i>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Aviation_(Warner_Robins)" title="Museum of Aviation (Warner Robins)">
        Museum of Aviation
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Robins_Air_Force_Base" title="Robins Air Force Base">
        Robins Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Warner_Robins,_Georgia" title="Warner Robins, Georgia">
        Warner Robins, Georgia
       </a>
       , a former
       <a href="/wiki/Air_National_Guard" title="Air National Guard">
        Air National Guard
       </a>
       B-1B base. This aircraft was the sixth B-1 produced, and was delivered to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/96th_Bomb_Wing" title="96th Bomb Wing">
        96th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       at Dyess AFB, Texas on 13 March 1986. This aircraft arrived at Robins AFB in September 2002. Robins AFB was previously home to one of two Air National Guard B-1B wings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         152
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0070
       <i>
        7 Wishes
       </i>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Hill_Aerospace_Museum" title="Hill Aerospace Museum">
        Hill Aerospace Museum
       </a>
       at
       <a href="/wiki/Hill_Air_Force_Base" title="Hill Air Force Base">
        Hill Air Force Base
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Ogden,_Utah" title="Ogden, Utah">
        Ogden, Utah
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         153
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ogden_Air_Logistics_Center" title="Ogden Air Logistics Center">
        Ogden Air Logistics Center
       </a>
       at Hill AFB performs depot level maintenance on the entire B-1 fleet.
      </li>
      <li>
       83-0071
       <i>
        Spit Fire
       </i>
       - near the main gate at
       <a href="/wiki/Tinker_Air_Force_Base" title="Tinker Air Force Base">
        Tinker Air Force Base
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">
        Oklahoma
       </a>
       . This aircraft was one of two that suffered an in-flight engine failure in 1990 that led to grounding of the fleet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         154
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>

       <i>
        Boss Hawg
       </i>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_the_United_States_Air_Force" title="National Museum of the United States Air Force">
        National Museum of the United States Air Force
       </a>
       at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wright-Patterson_AFB" title="Wright-Patterson AFB">
        Wright-Patterson AFB
       </a>
       near
       <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">
        Dayton, Ohio
       </a>
       . It is displayed in the Museum's Cold War Gallery, and replaces the B-1A (74-0174) formerly on display.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">

         <span>

          ]

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">
       Accidents and incidents
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Accidents and incidents">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Ten B-1s have been lost due to accidents. Between 1984 and 2001, 17 crew members and people on board have been killed in B-1 accidents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AP-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Crashes">
       Crashes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Crashes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       On 29 August 1984, B-1A (AF Ser. No. 74-0159) stalled and crashed while performing minimum control speed tests at low altitude. The crew used the
       <a href="/wiki/Escape_crew_capsule" title="Escape crew capsule">
        escape capsule
       </a>
       to leave the bomber, but the parachutes deployed improperly, causing the capsule to hit nose down. The impact killed the B-1's pilot, Rockwell test pilot Doug Benefield, and seriously injured two other crew members.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p63_158-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p63-158">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         157
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         158
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In September 1987, B-1B (s/n 84-0052) from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/96th_Bomb_Wing" title="96th Bomb Wing">
        96th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 338th Combat Crew Training Squadron,
       <a href="/wiki/Dyess_Air_Force_Base" title="Dyess Air Force Base">
        Dyess AFB
       </a>
       crashed near
       <a href="/wiki/La_Junta,_Colorado" title="La Junta, Colorado">
        La Junta, Colorado
       </a>
       while flying on a low-level training route. This was the only B-1B crash to occur with six crew members aboard. The two crew members in
       <a href="/wiki/Jump_seat" title="Jump seat">
        jump seats
       </a>
       , and one of the four crew members in
       <a href="/wiki/Ejection_seat" title="Ejection seat">
        ejection seats
       </a>
       perished. The root cause of the accident was thought to be a
       <a href="/wiki/Bird_strike" title="Bird strike">
        bird strike
       </a>
       on a wing's
       <a href="/wiki/Leading_edge" title="Leading edge">
        leading edge
       </a>
       during the low-level flight. The impact was severe enough to sever fuel and hydraulic lines on one side of the aircraft, the other side's engines functioned long enough to allow for ejection. The B-1B fleet was later modified to protect these supply lines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In November 1988, B-1B (s/n 85-0063) from the 96th Bomb Wing, 337th Bomb Squadron, Dyess AFB crashed near
       <a href="/wiki/Tye,_Texas" title="Tye, Texas">
        Tye, Texas
       </a>
       after a fire broke out above the left engines. All four crew members successfully ejected from the aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In November 1988, B-1B (s/n 85-0076) from the
       <a href="/wiki/28th_Bomb_Wing" title="28th Bomb Wing">
        28th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 37th Bomb Squadron, Ellsworth AFB crashed short of the runway at Ellsworth AFB during adverse weather. All four crew members ejected successfully.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In November 1992, B-1B (s/n 86-0106) from the
       <a href="/wiki/7th_Bomb_Wing" title="7th Bomb Wing">
        7th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 337th Bomb Squadron, Dyess AFB, Texas, flying on a low-level training flight crashed into a mountain near
       <a href="/wiki/Van_Horn,_Texas" title="Van Horn, Texas">
        Van Horn, Texas
       </a>
       . All four members of the crew were killed, and the cause was attributed to pilot error.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In September 1997, B-1B (s/n 85-0078) from the
       <a href="/wiki/28th_Bomb_Wing" title="28th Bomb Wing">
        28th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 37th Bomb Squadron, Ellsworth AFB,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">
        South Dakota
       </a>
       , flying in the Powder River Military Operating Area crashed 25 miles (40km) north of
       <a href="/wiki/Alzada,_Montana" title="Alzada, Montana">
        Alzada, Montana
       </a>
       . All four members of the crew were killed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
        <a href="#cite_note-161">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         160
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The review board found that the bomber struck the ground while performing a defensive maneuver.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
        <a href="#cite_note-162">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         161
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       On 18 February 1998, B-1B (s/n 84-0057) from the 7th Bomb Wing, Dyess AFB, Texas crashed near
       <a href="/wiki/Marion,_Kentucky" title="Marion, Kentucky">
        Marion, Kentucky
       </a>
       when a fire detected by a cockpit instrument panel shut down the aircraft's power.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
        <a href="#cite_note-163">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         162
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       All four crew members were able to eject and were rescued safely.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B-1B_crash_Feb1998_164-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-B-1B_crash_Feb1998-164">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         163
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In December 2001, B-1B (s/n 86-0114) from the 28th Bomb Wing, 37th Bomb Squadron, Ellsworth AFB, South Dakota, was lost over the
       <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
        Indian Ocean
       </a>
       . All four crew members successfully ejected and were rescued.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
        <a href="#cite_note-165">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         164
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       The bomber was flying en route to a long-range combat mission over Afghanistan when the crew declared an in-flight emergency. The pilot, Captain William Steele, attributed the crash to "multiple malfunctions" causing the bomber to go "out of control".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_157-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AP-157">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         156
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of the water's depth, the structural data collector (SDC) or "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_data_recorder" title="Flight data recorder">
        Black Box
       </a>
       " was not recovered and the cause was not positively determined. The aircraft had recently returned from a routine Phase Inspection, and was on its first combat mission after returning to the island of
       <a href="/wiki/Diego_Garcia" title="Diego Garcia">
        Diego Garcia
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Ocean_Territory" title="British Indian Ocean Territory">
        British Indian Ocean Territory
       </a>
       . This was the first B-1B to be lost during combat operations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP_157-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-AP-157">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         156
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         165
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       On 4 April 2008, B-1B (s/n 86-0116) lost hydraulic power while taxiing, then crashed into a concrete barrier and caught fire at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Udeid_AB" title="Al Udeid AB">
        Al Udeid AB
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">
        Qatar
       </a>
       . The crew safely evacuated the aircraft. The B-1B was carrying two 2,000lb bombs at the time, which did not detonate. The aircraft was destroyed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
        <a href="#cite_note-167">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         166
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       On 19 August 2013, B-1B (AF Ser. No. 85-0091) out of Ellsworth AFB, South Dakota crashed in a remote area near Broadus, Montana. All four crew members survived by ejecting before the aircraft crashed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
        <a href="#cite_note-168">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         167
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       A report found that the crash was due to a mechanical failure leading to a fuel leak and explosions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
        <a href="#cite_note-169">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         168
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
        <a href="#cite_note-170">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         169
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_accidents_and_notable_incidents">
       Other accidents and notable incidents
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rockwell_B-1_Lancer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other accidents and notable incidents">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B1_fire.jpg">
        <img alt="Black aircraft trailed by column of black smoke and fire on runway as fire trucks close in on the flame from behind" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="640" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/B1_fire.jpg/220px-B1_fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/B1_fire.jpg/330px-B1_fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/B1_fire.jpg/440px-B1_fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B1_fire.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A B-1B with a brake fire after a hard landing at
        <a href="/wiki/Rhein-Main_Air_Base" title="Rhein-Main Air Base">
         Rhein-Main AB
        </a>
        , Germany, June 1994.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       On 4 October 1989, B-1B (s/n 85-0070) from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/96th_Bomb_Wing" title="96th Bomb Wing">
        96th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 337th Bomb Squadron, Dyess AFB, Texas, was forced to make an emergency landing at
       <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">
        Edwards Air Force Base
       </a>
       after suffering an in-flight failure of the No. 2 hydraulic system, which required use of the No 3 system deploy the landing gear. After the nose gear failed to deploy, the crew attempted to lower the nose gear via the alternate emergency gear-extension method. After several hours of attempting different means to lower the nose gear, the decision was made to perform a nose-gear-up landing on
       <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Dry_Lake" title="Rogers Dry Lake">
        Rogers Dry Lake
       </a>
       at Edwards. The lower portion of the forward bulkhead used to mount the radar antenna was the only part of the aircraft to suffer damage, and after repairs, the jet was returned to Dyess in January 1990 by the same crew which performed the emergency landing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
        <a href="#cite_note-171">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         170
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In October 1990, while flying a training route in eastern Colorado, B-1B (s/n 86-0128) from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/384th_Bomb_Wing" title="384th Bomb Wing">
        384th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 28th Bomb Squadron,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McConnell_AFB" title="McConnell AFB">
        McConnell AFB
       </a>
       , experienced an explosion as the engines reached full power without afterburners. Fire on the aircraft's left was spotted. The #1 engine was shut down and its
       <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher" title="Fire extinguisher">
        fire extinguisher
       </a>
       was activated. The accident investigation determined that the engine had suffered catastrophic failure, engine blades had cut through the engine mounts and the engine became detached from the aircraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In December 1990, B-1B (s/n 83-0071) from the 96th Bomb Wing, 337th Bomb Squadron, Dyess AFB, Texas, experienced a jolt that caused the #3 engine to shut down with its fire extinguisher activating. This event, coupled with the October 1990 engine incident, led to a 50+ day grounding of the B-1Bs not on
       <a href="/wiki/Second_strike#Implementation" title="Second strike">
        nuclear alert
       </a>
       status. The problem was eventually traced back to problems in the first-stage fan, and all B-1Bs were equipped with modified engines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_p114_160-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_p114-160">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         159
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In June 1994, B-1B (s/n 84-0057) from the
       <a href="/wiki/7th_Bomb_Wing" title="7th Bomb Wing">
        7th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/9th_Bomb_Squadron" title="9th Bomb Squadron">
        9th Bomb Squadron
       </a>
       , made an emergency divert to
       <a href="/wiki/Rhein-Main_Air_Base" title="Rhein-Main Air Base">
        Rhein-Main Air Base
       </a>
       , Germany due to a wing sweep malfunction. The crew made a high speed landing at Rhein-Main and stopped 100 feet short of the end of the runway. Afterward the overheated brakes led to a fire of the right main landing gear, but was quickly extinguished.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
        <a href="#cite_note-172">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         171
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       On 15 September 2005, B-1B (s/n 85-0066) was extensively damaged by fire while landing at
       <a href="/wiki/Andersen_Air_Force_Base" title="Andersen Air Force Base">
        Andersen Air Force Base
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
        Guam
       </a>
       . The investigation into the incident concluded that leaking hydraulic fluid and sparks from a wheel being gouged caused a fire to start in the aircraft's right main landing gear as it touched down. The resulting fire damaged the B-1's right wing, engine nacelle, airframe and landing gear, leading to an estimated repair cost of more than $32million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
        <a href="#cite_note-173">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         172
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       On 8 May 2006, B-1B (s/n 86-0132) from the
       <a href="/wiki/7th_Bomb_Wing" title="7th Bomb Wing">
        7th Bomb Wing
       </a>
       , 9th Bomb Squadron, Dyess AFB, Texas, landed "gear-up" during recovery from an 11-hour ferry flight to the island of Diego Garcia. A resulting fire was quickly extinguished and the crew escaped through the top hatch with only a minor back injury to the co-pilot. The air force investigation concluded that the crew "forgot to lower the
       <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">
        landing gear
       </a>
       " based on the following reasons: 1) co-pilot task oversaturation, 2) co-pilot's wanting to complete a long mission, 3) neither pilot completed the landing checklist, 4) co-pilot's belief that the pilot had lowered the landing gear when he had not, 5) pilot had turned over control to the co-pilot on the final approach and the pilot had reported to base that the landing gear was down when it was not - indicator lights showing the landing gear was still up were working and apparently ignored. As a result the B-1B impacted and slid on the runway, which caused approximately $8 million of damage to the aircraft and runway.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
        <a href="#cite_note-174">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         173
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
